Lorenzo stared at him. The man’s mind was a labyrinth, and he was mapping its every turn with terrifying speed. He was not accusing. He was *comprehending*. And in his comprehension, he was building a cage far stronger than any the Abbot could devise.

“What is the purpose of a beacon, Lorenzo?” Fra Michele asked softly.

“To warn. Or to guide.”

“And which is your lamb? A warning of a sin that dwells here? Or a guide to a… new understanding?” He let the question hang. “The Office of Sacred Doctrine has seen such things before. A nun who bleeds light from her stigmata. A fresco that weeps oil. Sometimes they are signs. Often they are the manifestations of a soul’s turbulent, misguided energy—a energy that seeks not union with God, but a signature upon the world. A declaration of self, disguised as devotion.”

He finally sat, opposite Lorenzo, folding his bird-bone hands on the table. “Your case is complex. There is the secular crime—the death in Florence. The Medici, I understand, have… interceded. For their own reasons. Your penance here satisfied a political need as much as a spiritual one. That is a thread we may leave tangled.” He paused. “Then there is the spiritual violation of your penance. You were to be silent, contemplative, passive. You have been none of these. You have been brilliantly, provocatively active. You have turned your punishment into a platform. That is a second thread.”

He leaned forward, the candlelight now catching in his winter-eyes. “But the third thread… the innovation. That is the thread I must follow. The Church is the bedrock of tradition. It is the *pietra forte* of faith. Innovation is the vein. Sometimes it carries water, a blessing. More often, it is a weakness, a fault line that leads to collapse. My duty is to determine which this is. To determine if the mind that conceived this thing is in alignment with God’s immutable truth, or in rebellion against it, seeking its own, new truths.”

“How?” The word escaped Lorenzo’s lips.

“You will finish it,” Fra Michele said.

Lorenzo’s breath caught. The vise again.

“The sun will rise tomorrow,” the Dominican continued, methodical, relentless. “The phenomenon will recur. I will witness it. The Abbot will witness it. Then, you will take up your tools. You will work. I will watch you work. I will watch the choices you make. The final form of the lamb—the expression on its face, the set of its body, the *intent* you carve into its last lines—will be your testimony. Not in words, which are slippery things. But in stone, which is honest. We will see if the finished work is a vessel of devotion, or a monument to the artist’s own cleverness. We will see if the innovation serves the tradition, or seeks to supplant it.”

He stood. “You will return to your workshop now. You will sleep, if you can. At dawn, you will carve. Under the eye of the sun, and under mine.”

It was not a reprieve. It was a deeper, more intimate trial. The silence was over. Now came the examination.

The walk back to the lean-to was a somnambulist’s journey. The night seemed thinner, bleached of mystery, reduced to a mere interval between one scrutiny and another. When the door closed behind him, the familiar space felt alien, a stage set for a performance whose script he did not know.

He did not look at the lamb. He lay on his pallet, staring at the thatch, feeling the ghost of the sun’s focused eye on his skin. He thought of Maestro Andrea. *The bronze is not an enemy to be conquered. It is a secret, waiting to be persuaded.* He had shouted at his fate, at his flaw, at his silence. And he had built something that shouted back. But Fra Michele did not fear the shout. He listened to its pitch, its timbre, to diagnose the soul of the shouter.

At first light, they came.

Fra Michele stood just inside the doorway, a still, dark column. Abbot Anselmo stood behind him, a portrait of anxious irrelevance. The morning sun, clean and sharp, sliced through the high, small window. It touched the lamb’s head.

For a long moment, nothing. Then, as the angle grew exact, a spark ignited in the depths of the parabolic eye. It was not the furious, concentrated beam of the day before—the sun was still too low, the air too hazy. It was a smouldering point of light, a live coal buried in the stone. It grew, intensifying, climbing the wall opposite. It became an oval, then a perfect, brilliant circle. A staring, luminous eye, projected onto the rough planks.

Brother Tommaso, who had crept up behind the Abbot, let out a stifled gasp and fell to his knees, his beads clicking furiously.

Fra Michele did not kneel. He did not cross himself. He observed. His head tilted slightly, his eyes narrowed, measuring the intensity, the precision of the circle. He watched as the eye of light began its slow, inevitable drift down the wall with the sun’s ascent.

“Geometry,” he murmured, as if confirming a hypothesis. Then he looked at Lorenzo. “Now. Work.”

The command was a key turning in a different lock. Lorenzo moved to his bench. His tools lay where he had left them. He picked up the *unghietto*, the fine, pointed chisel for detail. He approached the stone. The lamb’s face was nearly complete, save for the delicate folds around the eye, the subtle flare of a nostril, the curve of the upper lip. This was the territory of spirit. This was where a snarl or a smile was born. This was where a lamb could become meek, or serene, or knowing, or accusatory.

He could feel Fra Michele’s gaze on his hands, on the set of his shoulders, as palpable as the sunlight. The Abbot fidgeted. Tommaso prayed in a whisper. The Dominican was a void of pure attention.

Lorenzo placed the chisel’s point against the stone, just below the eye. He raised the small mallet.

He could carve meekness. The down-turned glance, the soft, accepting line of the mouth. The lamb as sacrifice, absorbing the world’s violence without comment. It would be beautiful. It would be traditional. It might be safe.

He brought the mallet down.

A tiny flake of stone curled away. Not meekness. He began to define the orbital bone, not to soften it, but to give it a subtle, underlying strength. The eye was not a passive receiver of light; it was the architect of the beam. The socket must suggest that.

*Tap.*

He moved to the mouth. Not a slack, gentle curve. A closed, firm line. Not a smile, not a frown. A seam. A silence. The silence of the lamb before the shearers. Not dumb, but *contained*.

*Tap.*

He worked for an hour. The only sounds were the precise, whispered blows of the mallet, Tommaso’s raspy breath, and the distant call of a rook in the cypress tree. He lost himself in the minute revelations of the stone, in the conversation between his intent and the *pietra forte*’s gritty resistance. He forgot the watching eyes. He was back in the silence, but a silence now charged with a terrible, clarifying pressure.

He was refining the line of the lamb’s back, the subtle S-curve of its spine, when Fra Michele spoke.

“Stop.”

Lorenzo’s hand froze in mid-air. He lowered the tools.

Fra Michele stepped forward. He circled the block once, twice, his gaze travelling over the newly finished details of the face, down the graceful, strong line of the neck, over the compact power of the haunches.

“Look at me,” Fra Michele said.

Lorenzo looked up. The Dominican’s face was unreadable.

“You have given it a spine,” Fra Michele said. “You have given its silence a quality of choice. Its acceptance looks like endurance. Its meekness has a backbone of stone.” He paused. “This is not the *Agnus Dei* of the illuminated manuscript. This is a lamb that has met the wolf and has not looked away. This is a lamb that understands the nature of the altar.”

He turned to Abbot Anselmo. “There is no heresy here. No diabolical trick. Only a profound and troubling ambiguity.”

The Abbot’s shoulders slumped with a confused relief. “Then… the penance is complete? He can go?”

Fra Michele’s winter-eyes returned to Lorenzo. “The secular crime is not my domain. The Medici’s arrangement stands. But the spiritual inquiry… is concluded. The innovation is not in service of the self. It is in service of a question. A question carved in stone and light.” He seemed almost disappointed, as if a fascinating, dangerous animal had proven less wild than he’d hoped. “The work is a paradox. A traditional form, innovated to make a statement that is neither traditional nor revolutionary. It is… personal. And the Church has no doctrine for the personal. It only has doctrine for the communal. We can condemn the communal error. We can only watch the personal mystery with… caution.”

He gathered his robes about him. “Finish it. Give it the final polish. Then you will leave this place. The stone will remain. A curiosity. A testament to a silence that spoke too loudly in the end.”

He left then, without another word, his footsteps fading on the path. Abbot Anselmo, after a long, bewildered look at the lamb, followed, pulling a trembling Brother Tommaso with him.

Lorenzo was alone.

The sun was high now. The eye of light on the wall had dissolved into a general, bright patch. The phenomenon was over. The scrutiny was over.

He felt empty. Scoured out. The defiance that had fuelled him was gone, its purpose spent. The trap had sprung, and he had neither been caught nor set free. He had been… assessed, and filed away.

He worked for three more days. He did not carve now; he polished. With pumice, with water, with the softest leather, he brought the *pietra forte* to life. Under his hands, the grey stone warmed, revealing subtle hues of gold and rust within its matrix. The lamb emerged from haze into hyper-clarity. Every muscle under the fleece, every tendon in the tucked legs, the firm, closed line of the mouth, the deep, parabolic eye-socket—all gleamed with a quiet, internal authority. It was done.

On the morning of the fourth day, a lay brother brought him a coarse wool tunic, hose, and a pair of worn boots. His own clothes from Florence, long stored, smelled of cedar and mothballs and another life. He changed in the lean-to, folding the monk’s habit neatly on his pallet.

He stood before the finished lamb one last time. The morning light played over its polished form, but it cast no accusing eye. It simply was. A lamb. His lamb. Born from a flaw, a silence, and a violence. It was neither a plea for forgiveness nor a declaration of innocence. It was a record. A fossil of a specific, pressurized time.

He touched its head once, his fingers tracing the smooth, cool curve of its skull. Then he turned and walked out of the lean-to, leaving the door open behind him.

He was led not to the gate, but to the Abbot’s study. Anselmo was there, looking older. On the table lay a small purse and a sealed letter.

“The purse is from the monastery. For your labour,” the Abbot said, not meeting his eye. “The letter is from Florence. It came for you weeks ago. Under the terms of your silence, it was not delivered.”

Lorenzo picked up the letter. The seal was not a Medici palle. It was a simple, plain blob of wax. He broke it.

*Lorenzo,*

*The Maestro is gone. To France, they say. The bottega is scattered. I am in Prato, working for a wool merchant who needs a man who can draw straight lines and keep complex accounts. It is dull work. The air smells of lanolin and ledger dust, not turpentine or stone.*

*I heard a rumour you were in a monastery. Doing penance. I do not know the truth of what happened that night. I know only that Marco was a fool, and the world is less for your absence from it.*

*There is a chapel here, the Sacro Cingolo. They are looking for a man to oversee the maintenance of the frescoes. It is not carving. It is not the great work. But it is work with memory, with colour, with preserving the light that others have cast. It is a kind of silence, but one that listens to echoes.*

*If you ever have need of a place, and of a friend who remembers the sound of a chisel on bronze,*

*Giuliano*

Lorenzo folded the letter. The words were a hand, extended in the dark. Not upwards, towards glory, but sideways, towards simple continuity.

He tucked the letter and the purse inside his tunic. He gave the Abbot a short nod, then turned and walked down the long corridor, through the cloister, and to the great oak gate.

The porter, without a word, drew back the heavy iron bolt and swung the door inward.

The world outside was a shock of colour and sound. The road was dusty, alive with the buzz of insects, the call of a hawk high above. The hills rolled away, green and gold, under a vast, indifferent sky. He stood on the threshold, the monastery’s shadow at his back.

He took a step. Then another. The dust of the road puffed around his boots. He did not look back. There was nothing to see. The work was done. The silence was broken. The flaw had found its form.

He walked toward the distant, hazy line of the hills, toward the road that would lead, eventually, to Prato. To a chapel that held echoes. To a friend who remembered the sound of a beginning.

He was a man with a letter in his pocket, the dust of a boundary on his skin, and the memory of a lamb that had turned silence into light. He was not the man who had entered. He was the man the stone had carved.

And ahead, the road was just a road, waiting for the persuasion of his steps.
